<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Sheep</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Domesticated ruminant bred for meat, wool, and milk</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about the domesticated species of sheep. For other species, see <a href="/wiki/Ovis" title="Ovis">Ovis</a>. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Sheep_(disambiguation)" title="Sheep (disambiguation)">Sheep (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>

<p><b>Sheep</b> or <b>domestic sheep</b>  (<i><b>Ovis aries</b></i>) are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Domesticated" title="Domesticated">domesticated</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ruminant" title="Ruminant">ruminant</a> <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammals</a> typically kept as <a href="/wiki/Livestock" title="Livestock">livestock</a>. Although the term <i>sheep</i> can apply to other species in the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Ovis" title="Ovis">Ovis</a></i>, in everyday usage it almost always refers to domesticated sheep. Like all ruminants, sheep are members of the <a href="/wiki/Order_(biology)" title="Order (biology)">order</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Artiodactyla" title="Artiodactyla">Artiodactyla</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Even-toed_ungulate" title="Even-toed ungulate">even-toed ungulates</a>. Numbering a little over one billion, domestic sheep are also the most numerous species of sheep. An adult female is referred to as a <i>ewe</i> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/j/: 'y' in 'yes'">j</span><span title="/uː/: 'oo' in 'goose'">uː</span></span>/</a></span></span>), an intact male as a <i>ram</i>, occasionally a <i>tup</i>, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Castrated" title="Castrated">castrated</a> male as a <i>wether</i>, and a young sheep as a <i>lamb</i>.
</p><p>Sheep are most likely descended from the wild <a href="/wiki/Mouflon" title="Mouflon">mouflon</a> of Europe and Asia, with Iran being a geographic envelope of the domestication center.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> One of the earliest animals to be domesticated for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Agricultural" title="Agricultural">agricultural</a> purposes, sheep are raised for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fleeces" title="Fleeces">fleeces</a>, meat (lamb, hogget or mutton) and <a href="/wiki/Sheep_milk" title="Sheep milk">milk</a>. A sheep's <a href="/wiki/Wool" title="Wool">wool</a> is the most widely used animal fiber, and is usually harvested by <a href="/wiki/Sheep_shearing" title="Sheep shearing">shearing</a>. In Commonwealth countries, <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/ovine" title="wikt:ovine">ovine</a> meat is called <a href="/wiki/Lamb_and_mutton" title="Lamb and mutton">lamb</a> when from younger animals and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mutton" title="Mutton">mutton</a> when from older ones; in the United States, meat from both older and younger animals is usually called lamb. Sheep continue to be important for wool and meat today, and are also occasionally raised for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sheepskin_(material)" title="Sheepskin (material)">pelts</a>, as <a href="/wiki/Dairy" title="Dairy">dairy</a> animals, or as <a href="/wiki/Model_organism" title="Model organism">model organisms</a> for science.
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sheep_husbandry" title="Sheep husbandry">Sheep husbandry</a> is practised throughout the majority of the inhabited world, and has been fundamental to many civilizations. In the modern era, <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">New Zealand</a>, the southern and central <a href="/wiki/South_America" title="South America">South American</a> nations, and the <a href="/wiki/British_Isles" title="British Isles">British Isles</a> are most closely associated with sheep production.
</p><p>There is a large <a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_sheep_husbandry" title="Glossary of sheep husbandry">lexicon of unique terms</a> for sheep husbandry which vary considerably by region and <a href="/wiki/Dialect" title="Dialect">dialect</a>. Use of the word <i>sheep</i> began in <a href="/wiki/Middle_English" title="Middle English">Middle English</a> as a derivation of the <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a> word <i><span title="Old English (ca. 450-1100)-language text"><i lang="ang">scēap</i></span></i>; it is both the singular and plural name for the animal. A group of sheep is called a flock. Many other specific terms for the various life stages of sheep exist, generally related to lambing, shearing, and age.
</p><p>Being a key animal in the history of farming, sheep have a deeply entrenched place in human culture, and find representation in much modern language and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Symbology" title="Symbology">symbology</a>. As livestock, sheep are most often associated with <a href="/wiki/Pastoral" title="Pastoral">pastoral</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arcadia_(utopia)" title="Arcadia (utopia)">Arcadian</a> imagery. Sheep figure in many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mythologies" title="Mythologies">mythologies</a>—such as the <a href="/wiki/Golden_Fleece" title="Golden Fleece">Golden Fleece</a>—and major religions, especially the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abrahamic" title="Abrahamic">Abrahamic</a> traditions. In both ancient and modern religious ritual, sheep are used as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sacrificial_animals" title="Sacrificial animals">sacrificial animals</a>.
</p>

</div>
</div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_the_domestic_sheep" title="History of the domestic sheep">History of the domestic sheep</a></div>
<p>The exact line of descent from wild ancestors to domestic sheep is unclear.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hiendleder_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hiendleder-2">[2]</a></sup> The most common hypothesis states that <i>Ovis aries</i> is descended from the Asiatic (<i>O. gmelini</i>) species of <a href="/wiki/Mouflon" title="Mouflon">mouflon</a>; the <a href="/wiki/European_mouflon" title="European mouflon">European mouflon</a> (<i>Ovis aries musimon</i>) is a direct descendant of this population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ensminger_5_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ensminger_5-3">[3]</a></sup> Sheep were among the first animals to be domesticated by humankind (although the domestication of dogs probably took place 10 to 20 thousand years earlier); the domestication date is estimated to fall between 11,000 and 9,000 B.C in <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ensminger_4_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ensminger_4-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weaver_1114_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Weaver_1114-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-simmons_2_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-simmons_2-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> and possibly around 7,000 B.C. in <a href="/wiki/Mehrgarh" title="Mehrgarh">Mehrgarh</a> in the Indus Valley.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> The rearing of sheep for secondary products, and the resulting breed development, began in either southwest Asia or western Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> Initially, sheep were kept solely for meat, milk and skins. Archaeological evidence from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Statuary" title="Statuary">statuary</a> found at sites in <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a> suggests that selection for woolly sheep may have begun around 6000 BC,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ensminger_5_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ensminger_5-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weaver_11_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Weaver_11-11">[11]</a></sup> and the earliest woven wool garments have been dated to two to three thousand years later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith_8_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Smith_8-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p><p>Sheep husbandry spread quickly in  Europe. Excavations show that in about 6000 BC, during the <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> period of prehistory, the Castelnovien people, living around <a href="/wiki/Ch%C3%A2teauneuf-les-Martigues" title="Châteauneuf-les-Martigues">Châteauneuf-les-Martigues</a> near present-day <a href="/wiki/Marseille" title="Marseille">Marseille</a> in the south of France, were among the first in Europe to keep domestic sheep.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> Practically from its inception, <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">ancient Greek civilization</a> relied on sheep as primary livestock, and were even said to name individual animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weaver_13_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Weaver_13-14">[14]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Romans" title="Ancient Romans">Ancient Romans</a> kept sheep on a wide scale, and were an important agent in the spread of sheep raising. <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Pliny the Elder</a>, in his <a href="/wiki/Natural_History_(Pliny)" title="Natural History (Pliny)">Natural History</a> (<i>Naturalis Historia</i>), speaks at length about sheep and wool.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pliny_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-pliny-15">[15]</a></sup> European colonists spread the practice to the <a href="/wiki/New_World" title="New World">New World</a> from 1493 onwards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sheep_and_goat_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-sheep_and_goat-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weaver_12_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Weaver_12-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Characteristics">Characteristics</span></h2><p>
Domestic sheep are relatively small ruminants, usually with a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crimp_(wool)" title="Crimp (wool)">crimped</a> hair called wool and often with horns forming a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lateral_(anatomy)" title="Lateral (anatomy)">lateral</a> <a href="/wiki/Spiral" title="Spiral">spiral</a>. Domestic sheep differ from their wild relatives and ancestors in several respects, having become uniquely <a href="/wiki/Neoteny" title="Neoteny">neotenic</a> as a result of selective breeding by humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> A few primitive breeds of sheep retain some of the characteristics of their wild cousins, such as short tails. Depending on breed, domestic sheep may have no horns at all (i.e. <a href="/wiki/Polled_livestock" title="Polled livestock">polled</a>), or horns in both sexes, or in males only. Most horned breeds have a single pair, but a few breeds may have several.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sheep_and_goat_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-sheep_and_goat-16">[16]</a></sup></p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Turkmen_sheep.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1256" data-file-width="2320" decoding="async" height="119" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Turkmen_sheep.jpg/220px-Turkmen_sheep.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Turkmen_sheep.jpg/330px-Turkmen_sheep.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Turkmen_sheep.jpg/440px-Turkmen_sheep.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Turkmen_sheep.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sheep in <a href="/wiki/Turkmenistan" title="Turkmenistan">Turkmenistan</a></div></div></div>
<p>Another trait unique to domestic sheep as compared to wild ovines is their wide variation in color. Wild sheep are largely variations of brown hues, and variation within species is extremely limited. Colors of domestic sheep range from pure white to dark chocolate brown, and even spotted or <a href="/wiki/Piebald" title="Piebald">piebald</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rmcsba_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-rmcsba-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bcsba_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-bcsba-21">[21]</a></sup> Selection for easily dyeable white fleeces began early in sheep domestication, and as white wool is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dominant_trait" title="Dominant trait">dominant trait</a> it spread quickly. However, colored sheep do appear in many modern breeds, and may even appear as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Recessive" title="Recessive">recessive</a> trait in white flocks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rmcsba_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-rmcsba-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bcsba_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-bcsba-21">[21]</a></sup> While white wool is desirable for large commercial markets, there is a <a href="/wiki/Niche_market" title="Niche market">niche market</a> for colored fleeces, mostly for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Handspinning" title="Handspinning">handspinning</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hobby_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-hobby-22">[22]</a></sup> The nature of the fleece varies widely among the breeds, from dense and highly crimped, to long and hairlike. There is variation of wool type and quality even among members of the same flock, so <a href="/wiki/Wool_classing" title="Wool classing">wool classing</a> is a step in the commercial processing of the fibre.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Take_ours!.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Take_ours%21.jpg/220px-Take_ours%21.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Take_ours%21.jpg/330px-Take_ours%21.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Take_ours%21.jpg/440px-Take_ours%21.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Take_ours!.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Suffolk_sheep" title="Suffolk sheep">Suffolks</a> are a medium wool, black-faced breed of meat sheep that make up 60% of the sheep population in the U.S.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-storey_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-storey-23">[23]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Depending on breed, sheep show a range of heights and weights. Their rate of growth and mature weight is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heritable" title="Heritable">heritable</a> trait that is often selected for in breeding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-storey_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-storey-23">[23]</a></sup> Ewes typically weigh between 45 and 100 kilograms (100 and 220 lb), and rams between 45 and 160 kilograms (100 and 350 lb).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> When all deciduous teeth have erupted, the sheep has 20 teeth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> Mature sheep have 32 teeth. As with other ruminants, the front teeth in the lower jaw bite against a hard, toothless pad in the upper jaw. These are used to pick off vegetation, then the rear teeth grind it before it is swallowed. There are eight lower front teeth in ruminants, but there is some disagreement as to whether these are eight <a href="/wiki/Incisor" title="Incisor">incisors</a>, or six incisors and two incisor-shaped <a href="/wiki/Canine_tooth" title="Canine tooth">canines</a>. This means that the <a href="/wiki/Dentition" title="Dentition">dental formula</a> for sheep is either <span class="nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block; vertical-align:-0.5em; font-size:85%; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; padding:0 0.1em;" title="upper dentition: Incisors.Canines.Premolars.Molars">0.0.3.3</span><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; padding:0 0.1em; border-top:1px solid;" title="lower dentition: Incisors.Canines.Premolars.Molars">4.0.3.3</span></span></span> or <span class="nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block; vertical-align:-0.5em; font-size:85%; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; padding:0 0.1em;" title="upper dentition: Incisors.Canines.Premolars.Molars">0.0.3.3</span><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; padding:0 0.1em; border-top:1px solid;" title="lower dentition: Incisors.Canines.Premolars.Molars">3.1.3.3</span></span></span> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> There is a large <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diastema_(dentistry)" title="Diastema (dentistry)">diastema</a> between the <a href="/wiki/Incisor" title="Incisor">incisors</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Molars" title="Molars">molars</a>.
</p><p>
In the first few years of life one can calculate the age of sheep from their front teeth, as a pair of <a href="/wiki/Deciduous_teeth" title="Deciduous teeth">milk teeth</a> is replaced by larger adult teeth each year, the full set of eight adult front teeth being complete at about four years of age. The front teeth are then gradually lost as sheep age, making it harder for them to feed and hindering the health and productivity of the animal. For this reason, domestic sheep on normal <a href="/wiki/Pasture" title="Pasture">pasture</a> begin to slowly decline from four years on, and the life expectancy of a sheep is 10 to 12 years, though some sheep may live as long as 20 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sheep_and_goat_16-2"><a href="#cite_note-sheep_and_goat-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-begin_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-begin-28">[28]</a></sup> </p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cr%C3%A2ne_mouton.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="966" data-file-width="1452" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Cr%C3%A2ne_mouton.jpg/220px-Cr%C3%A2ne_mouton.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Cr%C3%A2ne_mouton.jpg/330px-Cr%C3%A2ne_mouton.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Cr%C3%A2ne_mouton.jpg/440px-Cr%C3%A2ne_mouton.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cr%C3%A2ne_mouton.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Skull</div></div></div><p>Sheep have good hearing, and are sensitive to noise when being handled.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-smith5_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-smith5-29">[29]</a></sup> Sheep have horizontal slit-shaped pupils, with excellent <a href="/wiki/Peripheral_vision" title="Peripheral vision">peripheral vision</a>; with visual fields of about 270° to 320°, sheep can see behind themselves without turning their heads.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hobby_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-hobby-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ASI_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-ASI-30">[30]</a></sup> Many breeds have only short hair on the face, and some have facial wool (if any) confined to the poll and or the area of the mandibular angle; the wide angles of peripheral vision apply to these breeds. A few breeds tend to have considerable wool on the face; for some individuals of these breeds, peripheral vision may be greatly reduced by "wool blindness", unless recently shorn about the face.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> Sheep have poor <a href="/wiki/Depth_perception" title="Depth perception">depth perception</a>; shadows and dips in the ground may cause sheep to baulk. In general, sheep have a tendency to move out of the dark and into well-lit areas,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-modern_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-modern-32">[32]</a></sup> and prefer to move uphill when disturbed. Sheep also have an excellent sense of smell, and, like all species of their genus, have <a href="/wiki/Scent_gland" title="Scent gland">scent glands</a> just in front of the eyes, and interdigitally on the feet. The purpose of these glands is uncertain,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith4_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Smith4-33">[33]</a></sup> but those on the face may be used in breeding behaviors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-storey_23-2"><a href="#cite_note-storey-23">[23]</a></sup> The foot glands might also be related to reproduction,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-storey_23-3"><a href="#cite_note-storey-23">[23]</a></sup> but alternative functions, such as secretion of a waste product or a scent marker to help lost sheep find their flock, have also been proposed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith4_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-Smith4-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p><h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Comparison_with_goats">Comparison with goats</span></h3>
<p>Sheep and <a href="/wiki/Goat" title="Goat">goats</a> are closely related: both are in the subfamily <a href="/wiki/Caprinae" title="Caprinae">Caprinae</a>. However, they are separate species, so <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_(biology)" title="Hybrid (biology)">hybrids</a> rarely occur and are always infertile. A hybrid of a ewe and a buck (a male goat) is called a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sheep-goat_hybrid" title="Sheep-goat hybrid">sheep-goat hybrid</a>, known as <i>geep</i>. Visual differences between sheep and goats include the beard of goats and divided upper lip of sheep. Sheep tails also hang down, even when short or <a href="/wiki/Docking_(animal)" title="Docking (animal)">docked</a>, while the short tails of goats are held upwards. Also, sheep breeds are often naturally <a href="/wiki/Polled_livestock" title="Polled livestock">polled</a> (either in both sexes or just in the female), while naturally polled goats are rare (though many are polled artificially). Males of the two species differ in that buck goats acquire a unique and strong odor during the <a href="/wiki/Rut_(mammalian_reproduction)" title="Rut (mammalian reproduction)">rut</a>, whereas rams do not.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-begin_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-begin-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Breeds">Breeds</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Synchronized_Sheep_Judging.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="3872" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Synchronized_Sheep_Judging.jpg/220px-Synchronized_Sheep_Judging.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Synchronized_Sheep_Judging.jpg/330px-Synchronized_Sheep_Judging.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Synchronized_Sheep_Judging.jpg/440px-Synchronized_Sheep_Judging.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Synchronized_Sheep_Judging.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sheep being judged for adherence to their <a href="/wiki/Breed_standard" title="Breed standard">breed standard</a>.</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_sheep_breeds" title="List of sheep breeds">List of sheep breeds</a></div>
<p>The domestic sheep is a multi-purpose animal, and the more than 200 <a href="/wiki/List_of_sheep_breeds" title="List of sheep breeds">breeds</a> now in existence were created to serve these diverse purposes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sheep_and_goat_16-3"><a href="#cite_note-sheep_and_goat-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-okstate_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-okstate-34">[34]</a></sup> Some sources give a count of a thousand or more breeds,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dwyer2008_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dwyer2008-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jensen2009_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jensen2009-36">[36]</a></sup> but these numbers cannot be verified, according to some sources.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hobby_22-2"><a href="#cite_note-hobby-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-begin_28-2"><a href="#cite_note-begin-28">[28]</a></sup> However, several hundred breeds of sheep have been identified by the <a href="/wiki/Food_and_Agriculture_Organization" title="Food and Agriculture Organization">Food and Agriculture Organization of the UN</a> (FAO), with the estimated number varying somewhat from time to time: e.g. 863 breeds as of 1993,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> 1314 breeds as of 1995<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> and 1229 breeds as of 2006.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FAO_2007_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-FAO_2007-39">[39]</a></sup> (These numbers exclude extinct breeds, which are also tallied by the FAO.) For the purpose of such tallies, the FAO definition of a breed is "either a subspecific group of domestic livestock with definable and identifiable external characteristics that enable it to be separated by visual appraisal from other similarly defined groups within the same species or a group for which geographical and/or cultural separation from phenotypically similar groups has led to acceptance of its separate identity."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FAO_2007_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-FAO_2007-39">[39]</a></sup> Almost all sheep are classified as being best suited to furnishing a certain product: wool, meat, milk, hides, or a combination in a dual-purpose breed. Other features used when classifying sheep include face color (generally white or black), tail length, presence or lack of horns, and the <a href="/wiki/Topography" title="Topography">topography</a> for which the breed has been developed. This last point is especially stressed in the UK, where breeds are described as either upland (hill or mountain) or lowland breeds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-modern_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-modern-32">[32]</a></sup> A sheep may also be of a <a href="/wiki/Fat-tailed_sheep" title="Fat-tailed sheep">fat-tailed type</a>, which is a dual-purpose sheep common in Africa and Asia with larger deposits of fat within and around its tail.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Barbados_Blackbelly.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="364" data-file-width="274" decoding="async" height="226" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Barbados_Blackbelly.JPG/170px-Barbados_Blackbelly.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Barbados_Blackbelly.JPG/255px-Barbados_Blackbelly.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/44/Barbados_Blackbelly.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Barbados_Blackbelly.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Barbados_Blackbelly" title="Barbados Blackbelly">Barbados Blackbelly</a> is a hair sheep breed of <a href="/wiki/Caribbean" title="Caribbean">Caribbean</a> origin.</div></div></div>
<p>Breeds are often categorized by the type of their wool. Fine wool breeds are those that have wool of great crimp and density, which are preferred for textiles. Most of these were derived from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Merino_(sheep)" title="Merino (sheep)">Merino</a> sheep, and the breed continues to dominate the world sheep industry. Downs breeds have wool between the extremes, and are typically fast-growing meat and ram breeds with dark faces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-D’Arcy_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-D’Arcy-40">[40]</a></sup> Some major medium wool breeds, such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Corriedale_sheep" title="Corriedale sheep">Corriedale</a>, are dual-purpose crosses of long and fine-wooled breeds and were created for high-production commercial flocks. Long wool breeds are the largest of sheep, with long wool and a slow rate of growth. Long wool sheep are most valued for crossbreeding to improve the attributes of other sheep types. For example: the American <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Columbia_(sheep)" title="Columbia (sheep)">Columbia</a> breed was developed by crossing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lincoln_(sheep)" title="Lincoln (sheep)">Lincoln</a> rams (a long wool breed) with fine-wooled <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rambouillet_(sheep)" title="Rambouillet (sheep)">Rambouillet</a> ewes.
</p><p>Coarse or <a href="/wiki/Carpet" title="Carpet">carpet</a> wool sheep are those with a medium to long length wool of characteristic coarseness. Breeds traditionally used for carpet wool show great variability, but the chief requirement is a wool that will not break down under heavy use (as would that of the finer breeds). As the demand for carpet-quality wool declines, some breeders of this type of sheep are attempting to use a few of these traditional breeds for alternative purposes. Others have always been primarily meat-class sheep.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-living_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-living-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:A_flock_of_Australian_White_Sheep_in_Mudgegonga,_Victoria,_Australia._This_is_breed_of_hair_sheep_suited_for_the_hot_and_varied_Australian_climate.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2648" data-file-width="3968" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/A_flock_of_Australian_White_Sheep_in_Mudgegonga%2C_Victoria%2C_Australia._This_is_breed_of_hair_sheep_suited_for_the_hot_and_varied_Australian_climate.jpg/220px-A_flock_of_Australian_White_Sheep_in_Mudgegonga%2C_Victoria%2C_Australia._This_is_breed_of_hair_sheep_suited_for_the_hot_and_varied_Australian_climate.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/A_flock_of_Australian_White_Sheep_in_Mudgegonga%2C_Victoria%2C_Australia._This_is_breed_of_hair_sheep_suited_for_the_hot_and_varied_Australian_climate.jpg/330px-A_flock_of_Australian_White_Sheep_in_Mudgegonga%2C_Victoria%2C_Australia._This_is_breed_of_hair_sheep_suited_for_the_hot_and_varied_Australian_climate.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/A_flock_of_Australian_White_Sheep_in_Mudgegonga%2C_Victoria%2C_Australia._This_is_breed_of_hair_sheep_suited_for_the_hot_and_varied_Australian_climate.jpg/440px-A_flock_of_Australian_White_Sheep_in_Mudgegonga%2C_Victoria%2C_Australia._This_is_breed_of_hair_sheep_suited_for_the_hot_and_varied_Australian_climate.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:A_flock_of_Australian_White_Sheep_in_Mudgegonga,_Victoria,_Australia._This_is_breed_of_hair_sheep_suited_for_the_hot_and_varied_Australian_climate.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A flock of <a href="/wiki/Australian_White_sheep" title="Australian White sheep">Australian White</a> hair sheep in Mudgegonga, Victoria, Australia. This is a new breed of hair sheep suited for the hot and varied Australian climate</div></div></div>
<p>A minor class of sheep are the <a href="/wiki/Dairy" title="Dairy">dairy</a> breeds. Dual-purpose breeds that may primarily be meat or wool sheep are often used secondarily as milking animals, but there are a few breeds that are predominantly used for milking. These sheep produce a higher quantity of milk and have slightly longer lactation curves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> In the quality of their milk, the fat and protein content percentages of dairy sheep vary from non-dairy breeds, but lactose content does not.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pulina2_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pulina2-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p><p>A last group of sheep breeds is that of fur or <b>hair sheep</b>, which do not grow wool at all. Hair sheep are similar to the early domesticated sheep kept before woolly breeds were developed, and are raised for meat and pelts. Some modern breeds of hair sheep, such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dorper_(sheep)" title="Dorper (sheep)">Dorper</a>, result from crosses between wool and hair breeds. For meat and hide producers, hair sheep are cheaper to keep, as they do not need shearing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-living_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-living-41">[41]</a></sup> Hair sheep are also more resistant to parasites and hot weather.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-begin_28-3"><a href="#cite_note-begin-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p><p>With the modern rise of corporate <a href="/wiki/Agribusiness" title="Agribusiness">agribusiness</a> and the decline of localized <a href="/wiki/Family_farm" title="Family farm">family farms</a>, many breeds of sheep are in danger of extinction. The <a href="/wiki/Rare_Breeds_Survival_Trust" title="Rare Breeds Survival Trust">Rare Breeds Survival Trust</a> of the UK lists 22 native breeds as having only 3,000 registered animals (each), and <a href="/wiki/The_Livestock_Conservancy" title="The Livestock Conservancy">The Livestock Conservancy</a> lists 14 as either "critical" or "threatened".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> Preferences for breeds with uniform characteristics and fast growth have pushed heritage (or heirloom) breeds to the margins of the sheep industry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-living_41-2"><a href="#cite_note-living-41">[41]</a></sup> Those that remain are maintained through the efforts of conservation organizations, breed registries, and individual farmers dedicated to their preservation.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Diet">Diet</span></h2>
<p>Sheep are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Herbivorous" title="Herbivorous">herbivorous</a> mammals. Most breeds prefer to graze on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grass" title="Grass">grass</a> and other short <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roughage" title="Roughage">roughage</a>, avoiding the taller woody parts of plants that goats readily consume.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pugh,_pp._19_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pugh,_pp._19-47">[47]</a></sup> Both sheep and goats use their <a href="/wiki/Lip" title="Lip">lips</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tongue" title="Tongue">tongues</a> to select parts of the plant that are easier to digest or higher in nutrition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pugh,_pp._19_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pugh,_pp._19-47">[47]</a></sup> Sheep, however, graze well in <a href="/wiki/Monoculture" title="Monoculture">monoculture</a> pastures where most goats fare poorly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pugh,_pp._19_47-2"><a href="#cite_note-Pugh,_pp._19-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rumen-sheep2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1697" data-file-width="2443" decoding="async" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Rumen-sheep2.jpg/220px-Rumen-sheep2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Rumen-sheep2.jpg/330px-Rumen-sheep2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Rumen-sheep2.jpg/440px-Rumen-sheep2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rumen-sheep2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ruminant system of a sheep</div></div></div>
<p>Like all ruminants, sheep have a complex <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Digestive_system" title="Digestive system">digestive system</a> composed of four chambers, allowing them to break down <a href="/wiki/Cellulose" title="Cellulose">cellulose</a> from stems, leaves, and seed hulls into simpler <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">carbohydrates</a>. When sheep <a href="/wiki/Grazing" title="Grazing">graze</a>, vegetation is chewed into a mass called a <a href="/wiki/Bolus_(digestion)" title="Bolus (digestion)">bolus</a>, which is then passed into the <a href="/wiki/Rumen" title="Rumen">rumen</a>, via the <a href="/wiki/Reticulum_(anatomy)" title="Reticulum (anatomy)">reticulum</a>. The rumen is a 19- to 38-liter (5 to 10 gallon) organ in which feed is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fermentation_(food)" title="Fermentation (food)">fermented</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-simmons146_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-simmons146-48">[48]</a></sup> The fermenting organisms include bacteria, fungi, and protozoa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> (Other important rumen organisms include some archaea, which produce methane from carbon dioxide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup>) The bolus is periodically regurgitated back to the mouth as <a href="/wiki/Cud" title="Cud">cud</a> for additional chewing and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Salivation" title="Salivation">salivation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-simmons146_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-simmons146-48">[48]</a></sup> After fermentation in the rumen, feed passes into the <a href="/wiki/Reticulum_(anatomy)" title="Reticulum (anatomy)">reticulum</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Omasum" title="Omasum">omasum</a>; special feeds such as grains may bypass the rumen altogether. After the first three chambers, food moves into the <a href="/wiki/Abomasum" title="Abomasum">abomasum</a> for final digestion before processing by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intestine" title="Intestine">intestines</a>. The abomasum is the only one of the four chambers analogous to the human stomach, and is sometimes called the "true stomach".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p><p>Other than forage, the other staple feed for sheep is <a href="/wiki/Hay" title="Hay">hay</a>, often during the winter months. The ability to thrive solely on pasture (even without hay) varies with breed, but all sheep can survive on this diet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-living_41-3"><a href="#cite_note-living-41">[41]</a></sup> Also included in some sheep's diets are <a href="/wiki/Mineral" title="Mineral">minerals</a>, either in a trace mix or in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Salt_lick" title="Salt lick">licks</a>. Feed provided to sheep must be specially formulated, as most cattle, poultry, pig, and even some goat feeds contain levels of <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a> that are lethal to sheep.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hobby_22-3"><a href="#cite_note-hobby-22">[22]</a></sup> The same danger applies to mineral supplements such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Salt_lick" title="Salt lick">salt licks</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ovca_u_naselju_Radini,_Istra_(Croatia).JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3777" data-file-width="5035" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Ovca_u_naselju_Radini%2C_Istra_%28Croatia%29.JPG/220px-Ovca_u_naselju_Radini%2C_Istra_%28Croatia%29.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Ovca_u_naselju_Radini%2C_Istra_%28Croatia%29.JPG/330px-Ovca_u_naselju_Radini%2C_Istra_%28Croatia%29.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Ovca_u_naselju_Radini%2C_Istra_%28Croatia%29.JPG/440px-Ovca_u_naselju_Radini%2C_Istra_%28Croatia%29.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ovca_u_naselju_Radini,_Istra_(Croatia).JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An unsheared sheep grazing in <a href="/wiki/Istria" title="Istria">Istria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Croatia" title="Croatia">Croatia</a></div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Grazing_behavior">Grazing behavior</span></h3>
<p>Sheep follow a <a href="/wiki/Diurnality" title="Diurnality">diurnal</a> pattern of activity, feeding from dawn to dusk, stopping sporadically to rest and chew their <a href="/wiki/Cud" title="Cud">cud</a>. Ideal pasture for sheep is not lawnlike grass, but an array of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grass" title="Grass">grasses</a>, <a href="/wiki/Legume" title="Legume">legumes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Forb" title="Forb">forbs</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> Types of land where sheep are raised vary widely, from pastures that are seeded and improved intentionally to rough, native lands. Common plants toxic to sheep are present in most of the world, and include (but are not limited to) cherry, some oaks and acorns, tomato, <a href="/wiki/Taxus" title="Taxus">yew</a>, rhubarb, potato, and <a href="/wiki/Rhododendron" title="Rhododendron">rhododendron</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Effects_on_pasture">Effects on pasture</span></h4>
<div class="boilerplate metadata plainlinks tfd" id="tfd" style="background-color: transparent; padding: 0; font-size:xx-small; color:#000000; text-align: center; border-bottom:1px solid #AAAAAA; overflow:hidden;">‹ The <a href="/wiki/Help:Template" title="Help:Template">template</a> below (<i><a href="/wiki/Template:Globalize" title="Template:Globalize">Globalize</a></i>) is being considered for merging. See <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Templates_for_discussion/Log/2022_October_6#Template:Globalize" title="Wikipedia:Templates for discussion/Log/2022 October 6">templates for discussion</a> to help reach a consensus. ›</div><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1097763485">.mw-parser-output .ambox{border:1px solid #a2a9b1;border-left:10px solid .mbox-image-div{width:52px}html.client-js body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .mbox-text-span{margin-left:23px!important}@media(min-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .ambox{margin:0 10%}}</style><table class="box-Globalize plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-globalize" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><img alt="Globe icon." data-file-height="290" data-file-width="350" decoding="async" height="40" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Ambox_globe_content.svg/48px-Ambox_globe_content.svg.png" systemic bias">worldwide view</a> of the subject</b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> You may <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Sheep&amp;action=edit">improve
