class="date">July 2022</span>)</i></span><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Sheep are largely <a href="/wiki/Grazing" title="Grazing">grazing</a> herbivores, unlike <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Browsing_(predation)" title="Browsing (predation)">browsing</a> animals such as goats and deer that prefer taller foliage. With a much narrower face, sheep crop plants very close to the ground and can <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Overgraze" title="Overgraze">overgraze</a> a pasture much faster than cattle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-begin_28-4"><a href="#cite_note-begin-28">[28]</a></sup> For this reason, many shepherds use <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Managed_intensive_rotational_grazing" title="Managed intensive rotational grazing">managed intensive rotational grazing</a>, where a flock is rotated through multiple pastures, giving plants time to recover.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-begin_28-5"><a href="#cite_note-begin-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-modern_32-2"><a href="#cite_note-modern-32">[32]</a></sup> Paradoxically, sheep can both cause and solve the spread of <a href="/wiki/Invasive_species" title="Invasive species">invasive plant species</a>. By disturbing the natural state of pasture, sheep and other livestock can pave the way for invasive plants. However, sheep also prefer to eat invasives such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cheatgrass" title="Cheatgrass">cheatgrass</a>, <a href="/wiki/Euphorbia_virgata" title="Euphorbia virgata">leafy spurge</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kudzu" title="Kudzu">kudzu</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spotted_knapweed" title="Spotted knapweed">spotted knapweed</a> over native species such as <a href="/wiki/Sagebrush" title="Sagebrush">sagebrush</a>, making grazing sheep effective for <a href="/wiki/Conservation_grazing" title="Conservation grazing">conservation grazing</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> Research conducted in <a href="/wiki/Imperial_County,_California" title="Imperial County, California">Imperial County, California</a> compared lamb grazing with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Herbicides" title="Herbicides">herbicides</a> for <a href="/wiki/Weed" title="Weed">weed</a> control in seedling <a href="/wiki/Alfalfa" title="Alfalfa">alfalfa</a> fields. Three trials demonstrated that grazing lambs were just as effective as herbicides in controlling winter weeds. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Entomologist" title="Entomologist">Entomologists</a> also compared grazing lambs to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Insecticides" title="Insecticides">insecticides</a> for insect control in winter alfalfa. In this trial, lambs provided insect control as effectively as insecticides.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Behavior">Behavior</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Border_Collie_sheepdog_trial.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="756" data-file-width="1733" decoding="async" height="96" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Border_Collie_sheepdog_trial.jpg/220px-Border_Collie_sheepdog_trial.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Border_Collie_sheepdog_trial.jpg/330px-Border_Collie_sheepdog_trial.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Border_Collie_sheepdog_trial.jpg/440px-Border_Collie_sheepdog_trial.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Border_Collie_sheepdog_trial.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sheep showing flocking behavior during a <a href="/wiki/Sheepdog_trial" title="Sheepdog trial">sheepdog trial</a></div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Flock_behavior">Flock behavior</span></h3>
<p>Sheep are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Herd_animal" title="Herd animal">flock</a> animals and strongly gregarious; much sheep behavior can be understood on the basis of these tendencies. The <a href="/wiki/Dominance_hierarchy" title="Dominance hierarchy">dominance hierarchy</a> of sheep and their natural inclination to follow a leader to new pastures were the pivotal factors in sheep being one of the first domesticated livestock species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wild_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-wild-57">[57]</a></sup> Furthermore, in contrast to the <a href="/wiki/Red_deer" title="Red deer">red deer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gazelle" title="Gazelle">gazelle</a> (two other ungulates of primary importance to meat production in prehistoric times), sheep do not defend <a href="/wiki/Territory_(animal)" title="Territory (animal)">territories</a> although they do form <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Home_ranges" title="Home ranges">home ranges</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CluttonBrock,_(1987)_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-CluttonBrock,_(1987)-58">[58]</a></sup> All sheep have a tendency to congregate close to other members of a flock, although this behavior varies with breed,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-smith5_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-smith5-29">[29]</a></sup> and sheep can become stressed when separated from their flock members.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-storey_23-4"><a href="#cite_note-storey-23">[23]</a></sup> During flocking, sheep have a strong tendency to follow, and a leader may simply be the first individual to move.  Relationships in flocks tend to be closest among related sheep: in mixed-breed flocks, subgroups of the same breed tend to form, and a ewe and her direct descendants often move as a unit within large flocks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hobby_22-4"><a href="#cite_note-hobby-22">[22]</a></sup> Sheep can become <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hefted" title="Hefted">hefted</a> to one particular local pasture (heft) so they do not roam freely in unfenced landscapes. Lambs learn the heft from ewes and if whole flocks are culled it must be retaught to the replacement animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-storey_23-5"><a href="#cite_note-storey-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p><p>Flock behaviour in sheep is generally only exhibited in groups of four or more sheep; fewer sheep may not react as expected when alone or with few other sheep.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hobby_22-5"><a href="#cite_note-hobby-22">[22]</a></sup> Being a prey species, the primary defense mechanism of sheep is to flee from danger when their <a href="/wiki/Flight_zone" title="Flight zone">flight zone</a> is entered. Cornered sheep may charge and butt, or threaten by hoof stamping and adopting an aggressive posture. This is particularly true for ewes with newborn lambs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hobby_22-6"><a href="#cite_note-hobby-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p><p>In regions where sheep have no natural predators, none of the native breeds of sheep exhibit a strong flocking behavior.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-begin_28-6"><a href="#cite_note-begin-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Herding">Herding</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Round_%27em_up.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="431" data-file-width="640" decoding="async" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Round_%27em_up.jpg/220px-Round_%27em_up.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Round_%27em_up.jpg/330px-Round_%27em_up.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Round_%27em_up.jpg/440px-Round_%27em_up.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Round_%27em_up.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Escaped sheep being led back to pasture with the enticement of food. This method of moving sheep works best with smaller flocks.</div></div></div>
<p>Farmers exploit flocking behavior to keep sheep together on unfenced pastures such as <a href="/wiki/Hill_farming" title="Hill farming">hill farming</a>, and to move them more easily. For this purpose shepherds may use <a href="/wiki/Herding_dog" title="Herding dog">herding dogs</a> in this effort, with a highly bred <a href="/wiki/Herding" title="Herding">herding</a> ability. Sheep are food-oriented, and association of humans with regular feeding often results in sheep soliciting people for food.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> Those who are moving sheep may exploit this behavior by leading sheep with buckets of feed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dominance_hierarchy">Dominance hierarchy</span></h3>
<p>Sheep establish a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dominance_(ethology)" title="Dominance (ethology)">dominance</a> hierarchy through fighting, threats and competitiveness. Dominant animals are inclined to be more aggressive with other sheep, and usually feed first at <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/trough" title="wikt:trough">troughs</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> Primarily among rams, horn size is a factor in the flock hierarchy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Budiansky78_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Budiansky78-64">[64]</a></sup> Rams with different size horns may be less inclined to fight to establish the dominance order, while rams with similarly sized horns are more so.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Budiansky78_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-Budiansky78-64">[64]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Merino" title="Merino">Merinos</a> have an almost linear hierarchy whereas there is a less rigid structure in Border Leicesters when a competitive feeding situation arises.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Squires_and_Daws,_(1975)_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Squires_and_Daws,_(1975)-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p><p>In sheep, position in a moving flock is highly correlated with social dominance, but there is no definitive study to show consistent voluntary leadership by an individual sheep.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Squires_and_Daws,_(1975)_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-Squires_and_Daws,_(1975)-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Intelligence_and_learning_ability">Intelligence and learning ability</span></h3>
<p>Sheep are frequently thought of as <a href="/wiki/Animal_cognition" title="Animal cognition">unintelligent animals</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> Their flocking behavior and quickness to flee and panic can make shepherding a difficult endeavor for the uninitiated. Despite these perceptions, a <a href="/wiki/University_of_Illinois_system" title="University of Illinois system">University of Illinois</a> <a href="/wiki/Monograph" title="Monograph">monograph</a> on sheep reported their intelligence to be just below that of pigs and on par with that of cattle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hobby_22-7"><a href="#cite_note-hobby-22">[22]</a></sup>
Sheep can recognize individual human and ovine faces and remember them for years; they can remember 50 other different sheep faces for over two years; they can recognize and are attracted to individual sheep and humans by their faces, as they possess similar specialized neural systems in the <a href="/wiki/Temporal_lobe" title="Temporal lobe">temporal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Frontal_lobe" title="Frontal lobe">frontal lobes</a> of their brains to humans and have a greater involvement of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Right_brain_hemisphere" title="Right brain hemisphere">right brain hemisphere</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nature_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-nature-67">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-minds_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-minds-68">[68]</a></sup> In addition to long-term facial recognition of individuals, sheep can also differentiate emotional states through facial characteristics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nature_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-nature-67">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-minds_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-minds-68">[68]</a></sup> If worked with patiently, sheep may learn their names, and many sheep are trained to be led by <a href="/wiki/Halter" title="Halter">halter</a> for showing and other purposes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hobby_22-8"><a href="#cite_note-hobby-22">[22]</a></sup> Sheep have also responded well to <a href="/wiki/Clicker_training" title="Clicker training">clicker training</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hobby_22-9"><a href="#cite_note-hobby-22">[22]</a></sup> Sheep have been used as pack animals; <a href="/wiki/Tibetan_people" title="Tibetan people">Tibetan</a> nomads distribute baggage equally throughout a flock as it is herded between living sites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hobby_22-10"><a href="#cite_note-hobby-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p><p>It has been reported that some sheep have apparently shown problem-solving abilities; a flock in <a href="/wiki/West_Yorkshire" title="West Yorkshire">West Yorkshire</a>, <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a> allegedly found a way to get over <a href="/wiki/Cattle_grid" title="Cattle grid">cattle grids</a> by rolling on their backs, although documentation of this has relied on anecdotal accounts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vocalisations">Vocalisations</span></h3>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1097025294">.mw-parser-output .side-box{margin:4px 0;box-sizing:border-box;border:1px solid #aaa;font-size:88%;line-height:1.25em;background-color:#f9f9f9}.mw-parser-output .side-box-abovebelow,.mw-parser-output .side-box-text{padding:0.25em 0.9em}.mw-parser-output .side-box-image{padding:2px 0 2px 0.9em;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .side-box-imageright{padding:2px 0.9em 2px 0;text-align:center}@media(min-width:500px){.mw-parser-output .side-box-flex{display:flex;align-items:center}.mw-parser-output .side-box-text{flex:1}}@media(min-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .side-box{width:238px}.mw-parser-output .side-box-right{clear:right;float:right;margin-left:1em}.mw-parser-output .side-box-left{margin-right:1em}}</style><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1096940132">.mw-parser-output .listen .side-box-text{line-height:1.1em}.mw-parser-output .listen-plain{border:none;background:transparent}.mw-parser-output .listen-embedded{width:100%;margin:0;border-width:1px 0 0 0;background:transparent}.mw-parser-output .listen-header{padding:2px}.mw-parser-output .listen-embedded .listen-header{padding:2px 0}.mw-parser-output .listen-file-header{padding:4px 0}.mw-parser-output .listen .description{padding-top:2px}.mw-parser-output .listen .mw-tmh-player{max-width:100%}@media(max-width:719px){.mw-parser-output .listen{clear:both}}@media(min-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .listen:not(.listen-noimage){width:320px}.mw-parser-output .listen-left{overflow:visible;float:left}.mw-parser-output .listen-center{float:none;margin-left:auto;margin-right:auto}}</style><div class="side-box side-box-right listen noprint">
<div class="side-box-flex">
<div class="side-box-image"><div class="center"><div class="floatnone"><img alt="" data-file-height="160" data-file-width="160" decoding="async" height="50" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/50px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/75px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/100px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></div></div></div>
<div class="side-box-text plainlist"><div class="haudio">
<div class="listen-file-header"><a href="/wiki/File:Sheep_bleating.ogg" title="File:Sheep bleating.ogg">A sheep bleating</a></div>
<div><audio controls="" data-durationhint="9" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" data-mwtitle="Sheep_bleating.ogg" id="mwe_player_0" preload="none" style="width:232px;" width="232"><source data-bandwidth="156020" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-title="Original Ogg file (156 kbps)" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/13/Sheep_bleating.ogg" type='audio/ogg; codecs="vorbis"'/><source data-bandwidth="221696" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="MP3" data-title="MP3" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/1/13/Sheep_bleating.ogg/Sheep_bleating.ogg.mp3" type="audio/mpeg"/></audio></div>
<div class="description">A sheep bleat</div></div></div></div>
<div class="side-box-abovebelow"><hr/><i class="selfreference">Problems playing this file? See <a href="/wiki/Help:Media" title="Help:Media">media help</a>.</i></div>
</div>
<p>Sounds made by domestic sheep include bleats, grunts, rumbles and snorts. Bleating ("baaing") is used mostly for contact communication, especially between dam and lambs, but also at times between other flock members.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lynch1992_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lynch1992-70">[70]</a></sup> The bleats of individual sheep are distinctive, enabling the ewe and her lambs to recognize each other's vocalizations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> Vocal communication between lambs and their dam declines to a very low level within several weeks after parturition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lynch1992_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lynch1992-70">[70]</a></sup> A variety of bleats may be heard, depending on sheep age and circumstances. Apart from contact communication, bleating may signal distress, frustration or impatience; however, sheep are usually silent when in pain. Isolation commonly prompts bleating by sheep.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dwyer2008b_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dwyer2008b-72">[72]</a></sup> Pregnant ewes may grunt when in labor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> Rumbling sounds are made by the ram during courting; somewhat similar rumbling sounds may be made by the ewe,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lynch1992_70-2"><a href="#cite_note-Lynch1992-70">[70]</a></sup> especially when with her neonate lambs. A snort (explosive exhalation through the nostrils) may signal aggression or a warning,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lynch1992_70-3"><a href="#cite_note-Lynch1992-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> and is often elicited from startled sheep.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Senses">Senses</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:A_curious_Welsh_Mountain_sheep_(Ovis_aries).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2672" data-file-width="4000" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/A_curious_Welsh_Mountain_sheep_%28Ovis_aries%29.jpg/220px-A_curious_Welsh_Mountain_sheep_%28Ovis_aries%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/A_curious_Welsh_Mountain_sheep_%28Ovis_aries%29.jpg/330px-A_curious_Welsh_Mountain_sheep_%28Ovis_aries%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/A_curious_Welsh_Mountain_sheep_%28Ovis_aries%29.jpg/440px-A_curious_Welsh_Mountain_sheep_%28Ovis_aries%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:A_curious_Welsh_Mountain_sheep_(Ovis_aries).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Welsh Mountain sheep</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lamb_09807-a.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1944" data-file-width="2592" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Lamb_09807-a.jpg/220px-Lamb_09807-a.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Lamb_09807-a.jpg/330px-Lamb_09807-a.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Lamb_09807-a.jpg/440px-Lamb_09807-a.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lamb_09807-a.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Lamb</div></div></div>
<p>In sheep breeds lacking facial wool, the visual field is wide. In 10 sheep (Cambridge, Lleyn and Welsh Mountain breeds, which lack facial wool), the visual field ranged from 298° to 325°, averaging 313.1°, with binocular overlap ranging from 44.5° to 74°, averaging 61.7°.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Piggins1996_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-Piggins1996-76">[76]</a></sup> In some breeds, unshorn facial wool can limit the visual field; in some individuals, this may be enough to cause "wool blindness". In 60 Merinos, visual fields ranged from 219.1° to 303.0°, averaging 269.9°, and the binocular field ranged from 8.9° to 77.7°, averaging 47.5°; 36% of the measurements were limited by wool,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hutson1980_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hutson1980-77">[77]</a></sup> although photographs of the experiments indicate that only limited facial wool regrowth had occurred since shearing. In addition to facial wool (in some breeds), visual field limitations can include ears and (in some breeds) horns,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hutson1980_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hutson1980-77">[77]</a></sup> so the visual field can be extended by tilting the head. Sheep eyes exhibit very low <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hyperopia" title="Hyperopia">hyperopia</a> and little <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Astigmatism_(eye)" title="Astigmatism (eye)">astigmatism</a>. Such visual characteristics are likely to produce a well-focused retinal image of objects in both the middle and long distance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Piggins1996_76-1"><a href="#cite_note-Piggins1996-76">[76]</a></sup> Because sheep eyes have no <a href="/wiki/Accommodation_(eye)" title="Accommodation (eye)">accommodation</a>, one might expect the image of very near objects to be blurred, but a rather clear near image could be provided by the <a href="/wiki/Tapetum_lucidum" title="Tapetum lucidum">tapetum</a> and large retinal image of the sheep's eye, and adequate close vision may occur at muzzle length.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Piggins1996_76-2"><a href="#cite_note-Piggins1996-76">[76]</a></sup> Good depth perception, inferred from the sheep's sure-footedness, was confirmed in "visual cliff" experiments;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hutson1980_77-2"><a href="#cite_note-Hutson1980-77">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> behavioral responses indicating depth perception are seen in lambs at one day old.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> Sheep are thought to have colour vision, and can distinguish between a variety of colours: black, red, brown, green, yellow and white.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alexander_and_Shillito,_(1978)_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-Alexander_and_Shillito,_(1978)-80">[80]</a></sup> Sight is a vital part of sheep communication, and when grazing, they maintain visual contact with each other.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kilgour,_(1977)_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kilgour,_(1977)-81">[81]</a></sup> Each sheep lifts its head upwards to check the position of other sheep in the flock. This constant monitoring is probably what keeps the sheep in a flock as they move along grazing. Sheep become stressed when isolated; this stress is reduced if they are provided with a mirror, indicating that the sight of other sheep reduces stress.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Parrott,_(1990)_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-Parrott,_(1990)-82">[82]</a></sup>
</p><p>Taste is the most important sense in sheep, establishing forage preferences, with sweet and sour plants being preferred and bitter plants being more commonly rejected. Touch and sight are also important in relation to specific plant characteristics, such as succulence and growth form.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Krueger,_et_al.,_(1974)_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-Krueger,_et_al.,_(1974)-83">[83]</a></sup>
</p><p>The ram uses his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vomeronasal" title="Vomeronasal">vomeronasal</a> organ (sometimes called the Jacobson's organ) to sense the pheromones of ewes and detect when they are in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Estrus" title="Estrus">estrus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> The ewe uses her vomeronasal organ for early recognition of her neonate lamb.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Reproduction">Reproduction</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Domestic_sheep_reproduction" title="Domestic sheep reproduction">Domestic sheep reproduction</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Emerging_lamb_cropped.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="281" data-file-width="378" decoding="async" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Emerging_lamb_cropped.jpg/220px-Emerging_lamb_cropped.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Emerging_lamb_cropped.jpg/330px-Emerging_lamb_cropped.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/8d/Emerging_lamb_cropped.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Emerging_lamb_cropped.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The second of twins being born.</div></div></div>
<p>Sheep follow a similar <a href="/wiki/Reproduction" title="Reproduction">reproductive</a> strategy to other herd animals. A group of ewes is generally mated by a single ram, who has either been chosen by a breeder or (in <a href="/wiki/Feral" title="Feral">feral</a> populations) has established dominance through physical contest with other rams.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-living_41-4"><a href="#cite_note-living-41">[41]</a></sup> Most sheep are <a href="/wiki/Seasonal_breeder" title="Seasonal breeder">seasonal breeders</a>, although some are able to breed year-round.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-living_41-5"><a href="#cite_note-living-41">[41]</a></sup> Ewes generally reach sexual maturity at six to eight months old, and rams generally at four to six months.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-living_41-6"><a href="#cite_note-living-41">[41]</a></sup> However, there are exceptions. For example, Finnsheep ewe lambs may reach puberty as early as 3 to 4 months, and Merino ewes sometimes reach puberty at 18 to 20 months.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> Ewes have <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Estrus" title="Estrus">estrus</a> cycles about every 17 days,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> during which they emit a scent and indicate readiness through physical displays towards rams. A minority of rams (8% on average) display a preference for <a href="/wiki/Homosexual_behavior_in_animals" title="Homosexual behavior in animals">homosexuality</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-nyt-88">[88]</a></sup> and a small number of the females that were accompanied by a male fetus <i>in utero</i> are <a href="/wiki/Freemartin" title="Freemartin">freemartins</a> (female animals that are behaviorally masculine and lack functioning <a href="/wiki/Ovary" title="Ovary">ovaries</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-freemartin_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-freemartin-89">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup>
</p><p>In feral sheep, rams may fight during the <a href="/wiki/Rut_(mammalian_reproduction)" title="Rut (mammalian reproduction)">rut</a> to determine which individuals may mate with ewes. Rams, especially unfamiliar ones, will also fight outside the breeding period to establish dominance; rams can kill one another if allowed to mix freely.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-living_41-7"><a href="#cite_note-living-41">[41]</a></sup> During the rut, even usually friendly rams may become aggressive towards humans due to increases in their hormone levels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-storey_23-6"><a href="#cite_note-storey-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p><p>After mating, sheep have a <a href="/wiki/Gestation" title="Gestation">gestation</a> period of about five months,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> and normal labor takes one to three hours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> Although some breeds regularly throw larger litters of lambs, most produce single or twin lambs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-storey_23-7"><a href="#cite_note-storey-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-quints_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-quints-95">[95]</a></sup> During or soon after labor, ewes and lambs may be confined to small <a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_sheep_husbandry" title="Glossary of sheep husbandry">lambing jugs</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> small pens designed to aid both careful observation of ewes and to cement the bond between them and their lambs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-modern_32-3"><a href="#cite_note-modern-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-living_41-8"><a href="#cite_note-living-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lamb_first_steps_(edited).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="680" data-file-width="945" decoding="async" height="158" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Lamb_first_steps_%28edited%29.jpg/220px-Lamb_first_steps_%28edited%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Lamb_first_steps_%28edited%29.jpg/330px-Lamb_first_steps_%28edited%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Lamb_first_steps_%28edited%29.jpg/440px-Lamb_first_steps_%28edited%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lamb_first_steps_(edited).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A lamb's first steps</div></div></div>
<p>Ovine <a href="/wiki/Obstetrics" title="Obstetrics">obstetrics</a> can be problematic. By selectively breeding ewes that produce multiple offspring with higher birth weights for generations, sheep producers have inadvertently caused some domestic sheep to have difficulty lambing; balancing ease of lambing with high productivity is one of the dilemmas of sheep breeding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> In the case of any such problems, those present at lambing may assist the ewe by extracting or repositioning lambs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-living_41-9"><a href="#cite_note-living-41">[41]</a></sup> After the birth, ewes ideally break the <a href="/wiki/Amniotic_sac" title="Amniotic sac">amniotic sac</a> (if it is not broken during labor), and begin licking clean the lamb.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-living_41-10"><a href="#cite_note-living-41">[41]</a></sup> Most lambs will begin standing within an hour of birth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-living_41-11"><a href="#cite_note-living-41">[41]</a></sup> In normal situations, lambs nurse after standing, receiving vital <a href="/wiki/Colostrum" title="Colostrum">colostrum</a> milk. Lambs that either fail to nurse or are rejected by the ewe require help to survive, such as bottle-feeding or fostering by another ewe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup>
</p><p>Most lambs begin life being born outdoors. After lambs are several weeks old, <a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_sheep_husbandry" title="Glossary of sheep husbandry">lamb marking</a> (<a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_sheep_husbandry" title="Glossary of sheep husbandry">ear tagging</a>, <a href="/wiki/Docking_(animal)" title="Docking (animal)">docking</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mulesing" title="Mulesing">mulesing</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Castrating" title="Castrating">castrating</a>) is carried out.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-living_41-12"><a href="#cite_note-living-41">[41]</a></sup> Vaccinations are usually carried out at this point as well. Ear tags with numbers are attached, or ear marks are applied, for ease of later identification of sheep. Docking and castration are commonly done after 24 hours (to avoid interference with maternal bonding and consumption of colostrum) and are often done not later than one week after birth, to minimize pain, stress, recovery time and complications.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> The first course of vaccinations (commonly anti-clostridial) is commonly given at an age of about 10 to 12 weeks; i.e. when the concentration of maternal antibodies passively acquired via colostrum is expected to have fallen low enough to permit development of active immunity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> Ewes are often revaccinated annually about 3 weeks before lambing, to provide high antibody concentrations in colostrum during the first several hours after lambing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kimberling,_C._V_1988_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kimberling,_C._V_1988-104">[104]</a></sup> Ram lambs that will either be slaughtered or separated from ewes before sexual maturity are not usually castrated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-modern_32-4"><a href="#cite_note-modern-32">[32]</a></sup> Objections to all these procedures have been raised by animal rights groups, but farmers defend them by saying they save money, and inflict only temporary pain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-storey_23-8"><a href="#cite_note-storey-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-living_41-13"><a href="#cite_note-living-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Health">Health</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Scrapie_testing.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2700" data-file-width="1769" decoding="async" height="259" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Scrapie_testing.jpg/170px-Scrapie_testing.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Scrapie_testing.jpg/255px-Scrapie_testing.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Scrapie_testing.jpg/340px-Scrapie_testing.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Scrapie_testing.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A veterinarian draws blood to test for resistance to scrapie</div></div></div>
<p>Sheep may fall victim to poisons, <a href="/wiki/List_of_infectious_sheep_and_goat_diseases" title="List of infectious sheep and goat diseases">infectious diseases</a>, and physical injuries. As a prey species, a sheep's system is adapted to hide the obvious signs of illness, to prevent being targeted by predators.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-storey_23-9"><a href="#cite_note-storey-23">[23]</a></sup> However, some signs of ill health are obvious, with sick sheep eating little, vocalizing excessively, and being generally listless.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> Throughout history, much of the money and labor of sheep husbandry has aimed to prevent sheep ailments. Historically, shepherds often created remedies by experimentation on the farm. In some developed countries, including the United States, sheep lack the economic importance for drug companies to perform expensive clinical trials required to approve more than a relatively limited number of drugs for ovine use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> However, extra-label drug use in sheep production is permitted in many jurisdictions, subject to certain restrictions. In the US, for example, regulations governing extra-label drug use in animals are found in 21 CFR (Code of Federal Regulations) Part 530.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> In the 20th and 21st centuries, a minority of sheep owners have turned to alternative treatments such as <a href="/wiki/Homeopathy" title="Homeopathy">homeopathy</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Herbalism" title="Herbalism">herbalism</a> and even <a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_medicine" title="Traditional Chinese medicine">traditional Chinese medicine</a> to treat sheep veterinary problems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hobby_22-11"><a href="#cite_note-hobby-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-storey_23-10"><a href="#cite_note-storey-23">[23]</a></sup> Despite some favorable <a href="/wiki/Anecdotal_evidence" title="Anecdotal evidence">anecdotal evidence</a>, the effectiveness of alternative veterinary medicine has been met with skepticism in <a href="/wiki/Scientific_journal" title="Scientific journal">scientific journals</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hobby_22-12"><a href="#cite_note-hobby-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-storey_23-11"><a href="#cite_note-storey-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> The need for traditional anti-parasite drugs and <a href="/wiki/Antibiotic" title="Antibiotic">antibiotics</a> is widespread, and is the main impediment to certified <a href="/wiki/Organic_farming" title="Organic farming">organic farming</a> with sheep.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-living_41-14"><a href="#cite_note-living-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>Many breeders take a variety of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Preventive_medicine" title="Preventive medicine">preventive measures</a> to ward off problems. The first is to ensure all sheep are healthy when purchased. Many buyers avoid outlets known to be clearing houses for animals culled from healthy flocks as either sick or simply inferior.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-storey_23-12"><a href="#cite_note-storey-23">[23]</a></sup> This can also mean maintaining a closed flock, and <a href="/wiki/Quarantine" title="Quarantine">quarantining</a> new sheep for a month. Two fundamental preventive programs are maintaining good nutrition and reducing stress in the sheep. Restraint, isolation, loud noises, novel situations, pain, heat, extreme cold, fatigue and other stressors can lead to secretion of cortisol, a stress hormone, in amounts that may indicate welfare problems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gregory,_N._G_1998_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gregory,_N._G_1998-110">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moberg,_G._P_2000._pp._1-21_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-Moberg,_G._P_2000._pp._1-21-112">[112]</a></sup> Excessive stress can compromise the immune system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moberg,_G._P_2000._pp._1-21_112-1"><a href="#cite_note-Moberg,_G._P_2000._pp._1-21-112">[112]</a></sup> "Shipping fever" (pneumonic mannheimiosis, formerly called pasteurellosis) is a disease of particular concern, that can occur as a result of stress, notably during transport and (or) handling.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> Pain, fear and several other stressors can cause secretion of epinephrine (adrenaline). Considerable epinephrine secretion in the final days before slaughter can adversely affect meat quality (by causing glycogenolysis, removing the substrate for normal post-slaughter acidification of meat) and result in meat becoming more susceptible to colonization by spoilage bacteria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gregory,_N._G_1998_110-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gregory,_N._G_1998-110">[110]</a></sup> Because of such issues, low-stress handling is essential in sheep management. Avoiding poisoning is also important; common poisons are <a href="/wiki/Pesticide" title="Pesticide">pesticide</a> sprays, inorganic <a href="/wiki/Fertilizer" title="Fertilizer">fertilizer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Motor_oil" title="Motor oil">motor oil</a>, as well as radiator coolant containing ethylene glycol.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sheep_with_orf.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="572" data-file-width="589" decoding="async" height="214" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Sheep_with_orf.jpg/220px-Sheep_with_orf.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Sheep_with_orf.jpg/330px-Sheep_with_orf.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Sheep_with_orf.jpg/440px-Sheep_with_orf.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sheep_with_orf.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A sheep infected with <a href="/wiki/Orf_(disease)" title="Orf (disease)">orf</a>, a disease transmittable to humans through skin contact</div></div></div>
<p>Common forms of preventive medication for sheep are <a href="/wiki/Vaccination" title="Vaccination">vaccinations</a> and treatments for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parasites" title="Parasites">parasites</a>. Both external and internal parasites are the most prevalent malady in sheep, and are either fatal, or reduce the productivity of flocks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-storey_23-13"><a href="#cite_note-storey-23">[23]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Worm" title="Worm">Worms</a> are the most common internal parasites. They are ingested during grazing, incubate within the sheep, and are expelled through the digestive system (beginning the cycle again). Oral anti-parasitic medicines, known as <a href="/wiki/Deworming" title="Deworming">drenches</a>, are given to a flock to treat worms, sometimes after worm eggs in the feces has been counted to assess infestation levels. Afterwards, sheep may be moved to a new pasture to avoid ingesting the same parasites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-modern_32-5"><a href="#cite_note-modern-32">[32]</a></sup> External sheep parasites include: lice (for different parts of the body), sheep <a href="/wiki/Hippoboscidae" title="Hippoboscidae">keds</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nose_bot" title="Nose bot">nose bots</a>, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Psorergates_ovis&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Psorergates ovis (page does not exist)">sheep itch mites</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Maggot" title="Maggot">maggots</a>. Keds are blood-sucking parasites that cause general malnutrition and decreased productivity, but are not fatal. Maggots are those of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bot_fly" title="Bot fly">bot fly</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blow-fly" title="Blow-fly">blow-fly</a>, commonly <i><a href="/wiki/Common_green_bottle_fly" title="Common green bottle fly">Lucilia sericata</a></i> or its relative <i>L. cuprina</i>. Fly maggots cause the extremely destructive condition of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flystrike" title="Flystrike">flystrike</a>. Flies lay their eggs in wounds or wet, manure-soiled wool; when the maggots hatch they burrow into a sheep's flesh, eventually causing death if untreated. In addition to other treatments, <a href="/wiki/Crutching" title="Crutching">crutching</a> (shearing wool from a sheep's rump) is a common preventive method. Some countries allow <a href="/wiki/Mulesing" title="Mulesing">mulesing</a>, a practice that involves stripping away the skin on the rump to prevent fly-strike, normally performed when the sheep is a lamb.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DPI_SOP_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-DPI_SOP-116">[116]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Model_Code_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-Model_Code-117">[117]</a></sup>  Nose bots are fly larvae that inhabit a sheep's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paranasal_sinus" title="Paranasal sinus">sinuses</a>, causing breathing difficulties and discomfort. Common signs are a discharge from the nasal passage, sneezing, and frantic movement such as head shaking. External parasites may be controlled through the use of <a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_sheep_husbandry" title="Glossary of sheep husbandry">backliners</a>, sprays or immersive <a href="/wiki/Sheep_dip" title="Sheep dip">sheep dips</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-storey_23-14"><a href="#cite_note-storey-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p><p>A wide array of bacterial and viral diseases affect sheep. Diseases of the hoof, such as <a href="/wiki/Foot_rot" title="Foot rot">foot rot</a> and foot scald may occur, and are treated with footbaths and other remedies. <a href="/wiki/Foot_rot" title="Foot rot">Foot rot</a> is present in over 97% of flocks in the UK.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> These painful conditions cause lameness and hinder feeding. Ovine <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Johne%27s_disease" title="Johne's disease">Johne's disease</a> is a wasting disease that affects young sheep. <a href="/wiki/Bluetongue_disease" title="Bluetongue disease">Bluetongue disease</a> is an insect-borne illness causing fever and inflammation of the <a href="/wiki/Mucous_membrane" title="Mucous membrane">mucous membranes</a>. <a href="/wiki/Ovine_rinderpest" title="Ovine rinderpest">Ovine rinderpest</a> (or <i>peste des petits ruminants</i>) is a highly contagious and often fatal <a href="/wiki/Virus" title="Virus">viral</a> disease affecting sheep and goats. Sheep may also be affected by primary<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> or secondary photosensitization. <a href="/wiki/Tetanus" title="Tetanus">Tetanus</a> can also afflict sheep through wounds from <a href="/wiki/Sheep_shearing" title="Sheep shearing">shearing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Docking_(animal)" title="Docking (animal)">docking</a>, <a href="/wiki/Castration" title="Castration">castration</a>, or vaccination. The organism also can be introduced into the reproductive tract by unsanitary humans who assist ewes during lambing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup>
</p><p>A few sheep conditions are transmissible to humans. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orf_(animal_disease)" title="Orf (animal disease)">Orf</a> (also known as scabby mouth, contagious ecthyma or soremouth) is a skin disease leaving lesions that is transmitted through skin-to-skin contact. Cutaneous <a href="/wiki/Anthrax" title="Anthrax">anthrax</a> is also called woolsorter's disease, as the spores can be transmitted in unwashed wool. More seriously, the organisms that can cause spontaneous enzootic <a href="/wiki/Abortion" title="Abortion">abortion</a> in sheep are easily transmitted to pregnant women. Also of concern are the <a href="/wiki/Prion" title="Prion">prion</a> disease <a href="/wiki/Scrapie" title="Scrapie">scrapie</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Virus" title="Virus">virus</a> that causes <a href="/wiki/Foot-and-mouth_disease" title="Foot-and-mouth disease">foot-and-mouth disease</a> (FMD), as both can devastate flocks. The latter poses a slight risk to humans. During the 2001 FMD pandemic in the UK, hundreds of sheep were culled and some rare British breeds were at risk of extinction due to this.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-storey_23-15"><a href="#cite_note-storey-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p><p>Of the 600,300 sheep lost to the US economy in 2004, 37.3% were lost to predators, while 26.5% were lost to some form of disease. Poisoning accounted for 1.7% of non-productive deaths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nass_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-nass-121">[121]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Predators">Predators</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Domestic_sheep_predation" title="Domestic sheep predation">Domestic sheep predation</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Coyotekilling.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="317" data-file-width="467" decoding="async" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Coyotekilling.jpg/220px-Coyotekilling.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Coyotekilling.jpg/330px-Coyotekilling.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Coyotekilling.jpg/440px-Coyotekilling.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Coyotekilling.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A lamb being attacked by <a href="/wiki/Coyote" title="Coyote">coyotes</a> with a bite to the throat</div></div></div>
<p>Other than parasites and disease, <a href="/wiki/Predation" title="Predation">predation</a> is a threat to sheep and the profitability of sheep raising. Sheep have little ability to defend themselves, compared with other species kept as livestock. Even if sheep survive an attack, they may die from their injuries or simply from panic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-storey_23-16"><a href="#cite_note-storey-23">[23]</a></sup> However, the impact of predation varies dramatically with region. In Africa, Australia, the Americas, and parts of Europe and Asia predators are a serious problem. In the United States, for instance, over one third of sheep deaths in 2004 were caused by predation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nass_121-1"><a href="#cite_note-nass-121">[121]</a></sup> In contrast, other nations are virtually devoid of sheep predators, particularly islands known for extensive sheep husbandry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-storey_23-17"><a href="#cite_note-storey-23">[23]</a></sup> Worldwide, <a href="/wiki/Canidae" title="Canidae">canids</a>—including the domestic dog—are responsible for most sheep deaths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-canids_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-canids-124">[124]</a></sup> Other animals that occasionally prey on sheep include: felines, bears, birds of prey, ravens and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Razorback" title="Razorback">feral hogs</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nass_121-2"><a href="#cite_note-nass-121">[121]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup>
</p><p>Sheep producers have used a wide variety of measures to combat predation. Pre-modern shepherds used their own presence, <a href="/wiki/Livestock_guardian_dog" title="Livestock guardian dog">livestock guardian dogs</a>, and protective structures such as barns and fencing. Fencing (both regular and <a href="/wiki/Electric_fence" title="Electric fence">electric</a>), penning sheep at night and lambing indoors all continue to be widely used.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-living_41-15"><a href="#cite_note-living-41">[41]</a></sup> More modern shepherds used guns, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Animal_trapping" title="Animal trapping">traps</a>, and poisons to kill predators,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> causing significant decreases in predator populations. In the wake of the environmental and conservation movements, the use of these methods now usually falls under the purview of specially designated government agencies in most developed countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup>
</p><p>The 1970s saw a resurgence in the use of livestock guardian dogs and the development of new methods of predator control by sheep producers, many of them non-lethal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-modern_32-6"><a href="#cite_note-modern-32">[32]</a></sup> Donkeys and <a href="/wiki/Guard_llama" title="Guard llama">guard llamas</a> have been used since the 1980s in sheep operations, using the same basic principle as livestock guardian dogs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-storey_23-18"><a href="#cite_note-storey-23">[23]</a></sup> Interspecific pasturing, usually with larger livestock such as cattle or horses, may help to deter predators, even if such species do not actively guard sheep.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-living_41-16"><a href="#cite_note-living-41">[41]</a></sup> In addition to animal guardians, contemporary sheep operations may use non-lethal predator deterrents such as motion-activated lights and noisy alarms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-storey_23-19"><a href="#cite_note-storey-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economic_importance">Economic importance</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Agricultural_economics" title="Agricultural economics">Agricultural economics</a></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1066479718">.mw-parser-output
